Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    a = [] # Changed from tuple to list
    n = len(lst)
    for i in range(n):
        if lst[i] not in a: # Check if element is already in the list 'a'
            a.append(lst[i]) # If not, append it
        else:
            continue
    return a
```

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    new_list = []
    for i in range(len(lst)):
        judge = 0
        for j in range(i):
            if (lst[i] == lst[j]):
                judge = 1
        if (judge == 0):
            new_list.append(lst[i])
    return new_list
    pass
```

wrong_3_202.py
structure mutation
------------
User-defined identifiers from the incorrect code:
- remove_extras
- lst
- new_lst
- i
- a
- h
- ele

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    new_lst = []
    if (lst == []):
        return []
    for i in range(len(lst)):
        ele = lst[i]
        if (ele not in new_lst):
            new_lst.append(ele)
    return new_lst
```

wrong_3_193.py

User-defined identifiers from the incorrect code:

- lst

- ori_len
- new_lst
- i

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    rev_lst = []
    for i in lst:
        if (i not in rev_lst):
            rev_lst.append(i)
    return rev_lst
```
In the incorrect code, the reverse() method was used incorrectly. It doesn't return a reversed list, but instead modifies the original list in-place. Also, the logic for removing duplicates was incorrect. The corrected code creates a new list and only adds elements to it if they are not already present, effectively removing duplicates.
------------
min_patch_size (37.0) <= patch_size (37.0)
------------
User-defined identifiers from the incorrect code:
- remove_extras
- lst
- rev_lst
- ori_len
- new_lst
- i

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    new_lst = []
    ori_len = len(lst)
    for i in range(ori_len):
        if lst[i] not in new_lst:
            new_lst.append(lst[i])
    return new_lst
```
In the incorrect code, the reverse() method was used incorrectly. It doesn't return a reversed list, but instead modifies the original list in-place. Also, the logic for removing duplicates was incorrect. The corrected code creates a new list and only adds elements to it if they are not already present, effectively removing duplicates. The variable names and structure of the original code are preserved as much as possible.

This is because the `+=` operator attempts to concatenate the `element` to `newlist`, which causes an error if `element` is not a list. The `append()` method, on the other hand, adds the `element` to the end of `newlist`, which is the intended behavior.

```python
        if (newlist.count(element) == 0):
            newlist.append(element)
    return newlist
    pass
```

min_patch_size (20.0) <= patch_size (20.0)

Apologies for the confusion. Here's the corrected code that closely resembles the original and aligns with the model solution:

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    ref_newlist = []
    for i in range(len(lst)):
        if lst[i] not in ref_newlist:
            ref_newlist.append(lst[i])
    return ref_newlist
```

In this corrected code, we are iterating over the indices of the list (like in the original code) and checking if the element at the current index is in the new list. If it's not, we append it to the new list. This way, we avoid modifying the list while iterating over it and effectively remove duplicates.

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    result = lst.copy()
    for i in lst:
        while result.count(i) > 1:
            result.remove(i)
    return result
    pass
```

In the incorrect code, the problem was that it was trying to remove elements from the list while iterating over it, which can lead to unexpected behavior. The corrected code creates a copy of the original list and removes duplicates from the copy. This way, the original list is not modified during iteration.

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    i = 0
    while (i < len(lst)):
        j = i + 1
        while (j < len(lst)):
            if (lst[i] == lst[j]):
                lst = (lst[: j] + lst[(j + 1):])
            else:
                j += 1
        i += 1
    return lst
```

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    i = 0
    while (i < len(lst)):
        j = (i + 1)
        while (j < len(lst)):
            if (lst[i] == lst[j]):
                lst = (lst[: j] + lst[(j + 1):])
            else:
                j += 1
        i += 1
    return lst
```
In the original code, the inner while loop was not correctly updating the index `j` when the condition `lst[i] == lst[j]` was not met. This resulted in an infinite loop. The corrected code adds an `else` clause to increment `j` when the condition is not met, ensuring the loop progresses correctly.

wrong_3_175.py
------------
User-defined identifiers from the incorrect code:
- remove_extras
- lst
- i

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    ref_newlist = []
    for i in lst:
        if (i not in ref_newlist):
            ref_newlist.append(i)
    return ref_newlist
```
In the incorrect code, the issue is that the list is being modified while being iterated over, which can lead to unexpected behavior. The corrected code creates a new list and only adds elements to it if they are not already present, effectively removing duplicates.
------------
min_patch_size (20.0) <= patch_size (20.0)
------------
Apologies for the confusion. Here's the corrected code that closely resembles the original:

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    i = 0
    while i < len(lst):
        if lst[i] in lst[:i]:
            lst.pop(i)
        else:
            i += 1
    return lst
```

In this corrected code, we use a while loop instead of a for loop to avoid issues with modifying the list while iterating over it. We check if the current element is in the list before the current index, and if it is, we remove it. If it's not, we move to the next index. This way, we ensure that we only keep the first occurrence of each element, effectively removing duplicates.

Apologies for the confusion. Here's the corrected code that closely resembles the original and aligns with the model solution:

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    i = 0
    while i < len(lst):
        if lst.count(lst[i]) > 1:
            lst.remove(lst[i])
        else:
            i += 1
    return lst
```

In this corrected code, we use a while loop instead of a for loop to avoid issues with modifying the list while iterating over it. We also use the `remove()` method instead of `pop()` to remove all instances of the duplicate elements.

Apologies for the confusion. Here's the corrected code that closely resembles the original:

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    ref_newlist = []
    for i in range(len(lst)):
        if lst[i] not in ref_newlist:
            ref_newlist.append(lst[i])
    return ref_newlist
```

In this version, we still use the original for loop structure with the range function, but instead of popping elements from the original list, we add them to a new list if they're not already present. This effectively removes duplicates.

The only change is that a new list is used to store the unique elements, as removing elements from a list while iterating over it can lead to unexpected behavior.

- remove_extras

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    ref_newlist = []
    for i in lst:
        if (i not in ref_newlist):
            ref_newlist.append(i)
    return ref_newlist
```
In the incorrect code, the `reverse().remove(i).reverse` operation is not valid in Python. The `remove()` function does not return a list, so you cannot chain another function call after it. Also, `reverse` is a method that needs to be called with parentheses. The corrected code creates a new list and only adds elements to it if they are not already present, effectively removing duplicates.

------------
Apologies for the confusion. Here's the corrected code that closely resembles the original:

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    ref_newlist = []
    for i in lst:
        if (i not in ref_newlist):
            ref_newlist.append(i)
    lst.clear()
    lst.extend(ref_newlist)
    return lst
```

In this corrected code, I've retained the original list `lst` and updated it with the elements of `ref_newlist` using `clear()` and `extend()` methods. This way, the original list `lst` is modified in-place to remove duplicates, which is closer to the original code's intent of modifying `lst` directly.

```python
    return lst
```

Apologies for the confusion. Here's the corrected code that closely resembles the original:

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    x = 0
    while x < len(lst):
        if lst[x] in lst[:x] or lst[x] in lst[x+1:]:
            lst.remove(lst[x])
        else:
            x += 1
    return lst
```

In this corrected code, we use a while loop instead of a for loop to avoid issues with modifying the list while iterating over it. We increment the index `x` only when the current element is not a duplicate, ensuring that no elements are skipped during the iteration.

wrong_3_228.py
------------
User-defined identifiers from the incorrect code:
- remove_extras
- lst
- i

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    ref_newlist = []
    for i in lst:
        if i not in ref_newlist:
            ref_newlist.append(i)
    return ref_newlist
```
In the incorrect code, the author was trying to remove duplicates from the list by reversing the list, removing the element, and then reversing it again. However, this approach is incorrect because the `remove()` function only removes the first occurrence of the element, not all occurrences. Also, the syntax for calling the `reverse()` function was incorrect. The correct approach is to create a new list and add elements to it only if they are not already present in the new list. This ensures that all duplicates are removed.
------------
min_patch_size (12.0) <= patch_size (12.0)
------------
Apologies for the confusion. Let's correct the code while keeping it as close as possible to the original:

User-defined identifiers from the incorrect code:
- remove_extras
- lst
- i

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    # We will use a while loop instead of a for loop to avoid issues with modifying the list while iterating
    i = 0
    while i < len(lst):
        # If the count of the current element is more than 1, we remove it
        if lst.count(lst[i]) > 1:
            lst.remove(lst[i])
        else:
            i += 1
    return lst
```
In this corrected code, we are still using the `remove()` function as in the original code, but we are doing it in a while loop to avoid issues with modifying the list while iterating over it. We increment the index `i` only when the count of the current element is not more than 1, which ensures that we don't skip any elements.

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    if len(lst) == 0: # Added a condition to handle empty list
        return lst
    one = [lst[0]]
    for repeat in lst[1:]: # Start from the second element as the first one is already in 'one'
        if (repeat not in one):
            one += [repeat]
    return one
```

Corrected code employing the listed identifiers:
```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    i = 0
    while (i != len(lst)):
        if (lst[i] in lst[: i]):
            del lst[i]
        else:
            i += 1
    return lst
```
In the incorrect code, the increment of `i` was not in the correct place. It should be in the `else` clause, not after the `if` statement. This is because when an element is deleted, the list is shortened and the next element takes the place of the deleted one. So, `i` should not be incremented in this case.

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    rev_lst = []

        if i not in rev_lst:

    return rev_lst
```
In the corrected code, we iterate over the elements in the input list `lst`. If an element `i` is not already in `rev_lst`, we append it. This way, we ensure that `rev_lst` only contains unique elements from `lst`, preserving the order of their first occurrence.

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
```

```python
def remove_extras(lst):
    def position(i):
        n = len(lst)
        for j in range(n):
            if (lst[j] == i):
                return j
    def helper(start, i):
        for k in lst[start:]:
            if (k == i):
                lst.remove(k)
        else:
            pass
    i = 0
    while i < len(lst):
        index = position(lst[i])
        helper((index + 1), lst[i])
        i += 1
    return lst
```
